     <h3 id="post-71645"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=71645" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Introducing arxiv-sanity">Introducing arxiv-sanity</a></h3> 
     <small>Sunday, September 18th, 2016</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><iframe width="450" height="253" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/S2GY3gh6qC8?feature=oembed" frameborder="0" allowfullscreen></iframe></p> 
      <p>Only a small part of Arxiv appears at: <a href="http://www.arxiv-sanity.com/" target="_blank">http://www.arxiv-sanity.com/</a> but it is enough to show the feasibility of this approach.</p> 
      <p>What captures my interest is the potential to substitute/extend the program to use other similarity measures. </p> 
      <p>Bearing in mind that <strong>searching is only the first step towards the acquisition and preservation of knowledge.</strong></p> 
      <p>PS: I first saw this in a tweet by <a href="https://twitter.com/BecomingDataSci" target="_blank">Data Science Renee</a>.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=746" rel="category">Archives</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=3" rel="category">Searching</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Introducing arxiv-sanity</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-69177 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-authoring-topic-maps category-deep-learning category-machine-learning category-similarity category-topic-maps"> 
     <h3 id="post-69177"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=69177" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Deep Learning: Image Similarity and Beyond (Webinar, May 10, 2016)">Deep Learning: Image Similarity and Beyond (Webinar, May 10, 2016)</a></h3> 
     <small>Friday, May 6th, 2016</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://go.dato.com/webinars/deep-learning-20160510" target="_blank">Deep Learning: Image Similarity and Beyond</a> (Webinar, May 10, 2016)</p> 
      <p>From the registration page:</p> 
      <blockquote> 
       <p class="p1">Deep Learning is a powerful machine learning method for image tagging, object recognition, speech recognition, and text analysis. In this demo, we’ll cover the basic concept of deep learning and walk you through the steps to build an application that finds similar images using an already-trained deep learning model.</p> 
       <h4>Recommended for:</h4> 
       <ul> 
        <li>Data scientists and engineers</li> 
        <li>Developers and technical team managers</li> 
        <li>Technical product managers</li> 
       </ul> 
       <p class="p1"><strong>What you’ll learn:</strong></p> 
       <ul class="ul1"> 
        <li class="li1">How to leverage existing deep learning models</li> 
        <li class="li1">How to extract deep features and use them using GraphLab Create</li> 
        <li class="li1">How to build and deploy an image similarity service using Dato Predictive Services</li> 
       </ul> 
       <p class="p1"><strong>What we’ll cover:</strong></p> 
       <ul class="ul1"> 
        <li class="li1">Using an already-trained deep learning model</li> 
        <li class="li1">Extracting deep features</li> 
        <li class="li1">Building and deploying an image similarity service for pictures<span style="font-size: 1em;">&nbsp;</span><span style="font-size: 1em;"></span></li> 
       </ul> 
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Deep learning has difficulty justifying its choices, just like human judges of similarity, but could it play a role in assisting topic map authors in constructing <strong>explicit</strong> decisions for merging?</p> 
      <p>Once trained, could deep learning suggest properties and/or values to consider for merging it has not yet experienced?</p> 
      <p>I haven’t seen any webinars recently so I am ready to gamble on this being an interesting one. </p> 
      <p>Enjoy!</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=71" rel="category">Authoring Topic Maps</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=879" rel="category">Deep Learning</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=170" rel="category">Machine Learning</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=4" rel="category">Topic Maps</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Deep Learning: Image Similarity and Beyond (Webinar, May 10, 2016)</span></span></p>

    <div class="post-68914 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-similarity category-topic-maps category-wikipedia"> 
     <h3 id="post-68914"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=68914" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Similar Pages for Wikipedia – Lateral – Indexing Practices">Similar Pages for Wikipedia – Lateral – Indexing Practices</a></h3> 
     <small>Saturday, April 23rd, 2016</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="https://chrome.google.com/webstore/detail/similar-pages-for-wikiped/ibabphmjpolljfillmhiikhdohbolado" target="_blank">Similar Pages for Wikipedia</a> (Chrome extension)</p> 
      <p>I started looking at this software <strong>with a mis-impression</strong> that I hope you can avoid.</p> 
      <p>I installed the extension and as advertised, if I am on a Wikipedia page, it recommends “similar” Wikipedia pages. </p> 
      <p>Unless I’m billing time, plowing through page after page of tangentially related material isn’t my idea of a good time.</p> 
      <p>Ah, but I confused “document” with “page.”</p> 
      <p>I discovered that error while reading <a href="https://lateral.io/docs/api#adding-documents" target="_blank">Adding Documents</a> at <a href="https://lateral.io/" target="_blank">Lateral</a>, which gives the following example:</p> 
      <p><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2016/04/lateral-add-doc-example.png"><img src="http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2016/04/lateral-add-doc-example.png" alt="lateral-add-doc-example" width="403" height="190" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-68915" srcset="http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2016/04/lateral-add-doc-example.png 403w, http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2016/04/lateral-add-doc-example-300x141.png 300w" sizes="(max-width: 403px) 100vw, 403px"></a></p> 
      <p>Ah! <strong>So “document” means as much or as little text as I choose to use when I add the document.</strong></p> 
      <p>Which means if I were creating a document store of graph papers, I would capture only the new material and not the inevitable a “graph consists of nodes and edges….”</p> 
      <p>There are pre-populatd data sets, <a href="https://lateral.io/docs/news-recommender" target="_blank">News</a> 350,000+ news and blog articles, updated every 15 mins; <a href="https://lateral.io/docs/arxiv-recommender" target="_blank">arXiv</a> 1M+ papers (all), updated daily; <a href="https://lateral.io/docs/pubmed-recommender" target="_blank">PubMed</a> 6M+ medical journals from before July 2014; <a href="https://lateral.io/docs/sec-recommender" target="_blank">SEC</a> 6,000+ yearly financial reports / 10-K filings from 2014; <a href="https://lateral.io/docs/wikipedia-recommender" target="_blank">Wikipedia</a> 463,000 pages which had 20+ page views in 2013.</p> 
      <p>I suspect the granularity on the pre-populated data sets is “document” in the usual sense size.</p> 
      <p>Glad to see the option to define a “document” to be an arbitrary span of text. </p> 
      <p>I don’t need to find more “documents” (in the usual sense) but more relevant snippets that are directly on point. </p> 
      <p>Hmmm, perhaps indexing at the level of paragraphs instead of documents (usual sense)?</p> 
      <p>Which makes me wonder why we index at the level of documents (usual sense) anyway? Is it simply tradition from when indexes were prepared by human indexers? And indexes were limited by physical constraints?</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=4" rel="category">Topic Maps</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1028" rel="category">Wikipedia</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Similar Pages for Wikipedia – Lateral – Indexing Practices</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-66890 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-deep-learning category-graphs category-similarity category-similarity-retrieval"> 
     <h3 id="post-66890"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=66890" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Webinar: Image Similarity: Deep Learning and Beyond (January 12th/Register for Recording)">Webinar: Image Similarity: Deep Learning and Beyond (January 12th/Register for Recording)</a></h3> 
     <small>Monday, January 11th, 2016</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://go.dato.com/webinars/deep-learning-20160112" target="_blank">Webinar: Image Similarity: Deep Learning and Beyond</a> by <a href="https://dato.com" target="_blank">Dato</a>.</p> 
      <p>From the webpage:</p> 
      <blockquote> 
       <p>In this talk, we will extract features from the convolutional networks applied to real estate images to build a similarity graph and then do label propagation on the images to label different images in our dataset.</p> 
       <p><strong>Recommended for:</strong></p> 
       <ul> 
        <li>Data scientists and engineers</li> 
        <li>Developers and technical team managers</li> 
        <li>Technical product managers</li> 
       </ul> 
       <p><strong>What you’ll learn:</strong></p> 
       <ul> 
        <li>How to extract features from a convolutional network using GraphLab Create</li> 
        <li>How to build similarity graphs using nearest neighbors</li> 
        <li>How to implement graph algorithms such as PageRank using GraphLab Create</li> 
       </ul> 
       <p><strong>What we’ll cover:</strong></p> 
       <ul> 
        <li>Extracting features from convolutional networks</li> 
        <li>Building similarity graphs using nearest neighbors</li> 
        <li>Clustering: kmeans and beyond</li> 
        <li>Graph algorithms: PageRank and label propagation</li> 
       </ul> 
      </blockquote> 
      <p>I had mixed results with webinars in 2015. </p> 
      <p>Looking forward to this one because of the coverage of similarity graphs.</p> 
      <p>From a subject identity perspective, how much similarity do you need to be the “same” subject?</p> 
      <p>If I have two books, one printed within the copyright period and another copy printed after the work came into the public domain, are they the same subject? </p> 
      <p>For some purposes yes and for other purposes not. </p> 
      <p>The strings we give web browsers, usually starting with “https://” these days, are crude measures of subject identity, don’t you think?</p> 
      <p>I say “the strings we give web browsers” as the efforts of TBL and his cronies to use popularity as a measure of success, continue their efforts to conflate URI, IRI, and URL into only URL. <a href="https://url.spec.whatwg.org/" target="_blank">https://url.spec.whatwg.org/</a> The simplification doesn’t bother me as much as the attempts to conceal it. </p> 
      <p>It’s one way to bolster a claim to have anyways been right, just re-write the records that anyone is likely to remember. I prefer my history with warts and all.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=879" rel="category">Deep Learning</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=89" rel="category">Graphs</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1125" rel="category">Similarity Retrieval</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Webinar: Image Similarity: Deep Learning and Beyond (January 12th/Register for Recording)</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-65961 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-computer-science category-searching category-similarity category-similarity-retrieval category-tf-idf"> 
     <h3 id="post-65961"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=65961" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to arXiv Sanity Preserver">arXiv Sanity Preserver</a></h3> 
     <small>Sunday, November 29th, 2015</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="https://github.com/karpathy/arxiv-sanity-preserver" target="_blank">arXiv Sanity Preserver</a> by Andrej Karpathy.</p> 
      <p>From the webpage:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>There are way too many arxiv papers, so I wrote a quick webapp that lets you search and sort through the mess in a pretty interface, similar to my <a href="http://cs.stanford.edu/people/karpathy/nips2014/" target="_blank">pretty conference format</a>.</p> 
       <p>It’s super hacky and was written in 4 hours. I’ll keep polishing it a bit over time perhaps but it serves its purpose for me already. The code uses Arxiv API to download the most recent papers (as many as you want – I used the last 1100 papers over last 3 months), and then downloads all papers, extracts text, creates tfidf vectors for each paper, and lastly is a flask interface for searching through and filtering similar papers using the vectors.</p> 
       <p>Main functionality is a search feature, and most useful is that you can click “sort by tfidf similarity to this”, which returns all the most similar papers to that one in terms of tfidf bigrams. I find this quite useful.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/arxiv-sanity.jpg"><img src="http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/arxiv-sanity-300x163.jpg" alt="arxiv-sanity" width="300" height="163" class="aligncenter size-medium wp-image-65962" srcset="http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/arxiv-sanity-300x163.jpg 300w, http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/arxiv-sanity-1024x559.jpg 1024w, http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/arxiv-sanity.jpg 1278w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px"></a></p> 
      <p>You can see this rather remarkable tool online at: <a href="https://karpathy23-5000.terminal.com/" target="_blank">https://karpathy23-5000.terminal.com/</a></p> 
      <p>Beyond its obvious utility for researchers, this could be used as a framework for experimenting with other similarity measures. </p> 
      <p>Enjoy!</p> 
      <p>I first saw this in a tweet by <a href="https://twitter.com/arnicas" target="_blank">Lynn Cherny</a>.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=185" rel="category">Computer Science</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=3" rel="category">Searching</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1125" rel="category">Similarity Retrieval</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1360" rel="category">TF-IDF</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on arXiv Sanity Preserver</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-65798 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-context category-context-models category-dimensions category-similarity category-subject-identity category-vectors category-word-meaning"> 
     <h3 id="post-65798"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=65798" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Infinite Dimensional Word Embeddings [Variable Representation, Death to Triples]">Infinite Dimensional Word Embeddings [Variable Representation, Death to Triples]</a></h3> 
     <small>Thursday, November 19th, 2015</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="" target="_blank">Infinite Dimensional Word Embeddings</a> by <a href="http://arxiv.org/find/stat/1/au:+Nalisnick_E/0/1/0/all/0/1" target="_blank">Eric Nalisnick</a> and <a href="http://arxiv.org/find/stat/1/au:+Ravi_S/0/1/0/all/0/1" target="_blank">Sachin Ravi</a>.</p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>We describe a method for learning word embeddings with stochastic dimensionality. Our Infinite Skip-Gram (iSG) model specifies an energy-based joint distribution over a word vector, a context vector, and their dimensionality, which can be defined over a countably infinite domain by employing the same techniques used to make the Infinite Restricted Boltzmann Machine (Cote &amp; Larochelle, 2015) tractable. We find that the distribution over embedding dimensionality for a given word is highly interpretable and leads to an elegant probabilistic mechanism for word sense induction. We show qualitatively and quantitatively that the iSG produces parameter-efficient representations that are robust to language’s inherent ambiguity.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Even better from the introduction:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>To better capture the semantic variability of words, we propose a novel embedding method that produces vectors with stochastic dimensionality. By employing the same mathematical tools that allow the definition of an <em>Infinite Restricted Boltzmann Machine</em> (Côté &amp; Larochelle, 2015), we describe ´a log-bilinear energy-based model–called the <em>Infinite Skip-Gram</em> (iSG) model–that defines a joint distribution over a word vector, a context vector, and their dimensionality, which has a countably infinite domain. During training, the iSGM allows word representations to grow naturally based on how well they can predict their context. This behavior enables the vectors of specific words to use few dimensions and the vectors of vague words to elongate as needed. Manual and experimental analysis reveals this dynamic representation elegantly captures specificity, <em>polysemy</em>, and <em>homonymy</em> without explicit definition of such concepts within the model. As far as we are aware, this is the first word embedding method that allows representation dimensionality to be variable and exhibit data-dependent growth.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Imagine a topic map model that “<strong>allow[ed] representation dimensionality to be variable and exhibit data-dependent growth.</strong>”</p> 
      <p>Simple subjects, say the sort you find at <a href="http://www.schema.org" target="blank">schema.org</a>, can have simple representations.</p> 
      <p>More complex subjects, say the notion of “person” in U.S. statutory law (no, I won’t attempt to list them here), can extend its dimensional representation as far as is necessary.</p> 
      <p>Of course in this case, the dimensions are learned from a corpus but I don’t see any barrier to the intentional creation of dimensions for subjects and/or a combined automatic/directed creation of dimensions. </p> 
      <p>Or as I put it in the title, <strong>Death to All Triples</strong>.</p> 
      <p>More precisely, not just triples but any pre-determined limit on representation.</p> 
      <p>Looking forward to taking a slow read on this article and those it cites. Very promising.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=53" rel="category">Context</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1356" rel="category">Context Models</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=656" rel="category">Dimensions</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=13" rel="category">Subject Identity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=259" rel="category">Vectors</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=843" rel="category">Word Meaning</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Infinite Dimensional Word Embeddings [Variable Representation, Death to Triples]</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-65589 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-r category-similarity category-similarity-retrieval category-text-mining"> 
     <h3 id="post-65589"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=65589" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Editors’ Choice: An Introduction to the Textreuse Package [+ A Counter Example]">Editors’ Choice: An Introduction to the Textreuse Package [+ A Counter Example]</a></h3> 
     <small>Tuesday, November 10th, 2015</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://digitalhumanitiesnow.org/2015/11/editors-choice-an-introduction-to-the-textreuse-package/" target="_blank">Editors’ Choice: An Introduction to the Textreuse Package</a> by Lincoln Mullen.</p> 
      <p>From the post:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>A number of problems in digital history/humanities require one to calculate the similarity of documents or to identify how one text borrows from another. To give one example, the <a href="http://viraltexts.org/" target="_blank">Viral Texts</a> project, by Ryan Cordell, David Smith, et al., has been very successful at identifying reprinted articles in American newspapers.<a href="http://kellenfunk.org/" target="_blank"> Kellen Funk</a> and I have been working on a text reuse problem in nineteenth-century legal history, <a href="http://lincolnmullen.com/blog/detecting-text-reuse-in-legal-documents/" target="_blank">where we seek to track</a> how codes of civil procedure were <a href="http://kellenfunk.org/field-code/" target="_blank">borrowed and modified</a> in jurisdictions across the United States.</p> 
       <p>As part of that project, I have recently released the <a href="https://github.com/ropensci/textreuse" target="_blank">textreuse package</a> for <a href="https://www.r-project.org/" target="_blank">R</a> to <a href="https://cran.rstudio.com/web/packages/textreuse/" target="_blank">CRAN</a>. (Thanks to <a href="http://www.noamross.net/" target="_blank">Noam Ross</a> for giving this package a <a href="https://github.com/ropensci/onboarding/issues/20" target="_blank">very thorough open peer review</a> for <a href="https://ropensci.org/" target="_blank">rOpenSci</a>, to whom I’ve contributed the package.) This package is a general purpose implementation of several algorithms for detecting text reuse, as well as classes and functions for investigating a corpus of texts. <strong>Put most simply, full text goes in and measures of similarity come out.</strong> (emphasis added) </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Kudos to Lincoln on this important contribution to the digital humanities! Not to mention the package will also be useful for researchers who want to compare the “similarity” of texts as “subjects” for purposes of elimination of duplication (called <em>merging</em> in some circles) for presentation to a reader.</p> 
      <p>I highlighted </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p><strong>Put most simply, full text goes in and measures of similarity come out.</strong></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>to offer a cautionary tale about the assumption that a high measure of similarity is an indication of the “source” of a text.</p> 
      <p><a href="http://louisiana.gov/" target="_blank">Louisiana</a>, my home state, is the only <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" target="_blank">civilian</a> jurisdiction in the United States. Louisiana law, more at one time than now, is based upon Roman law.</p> 
      <p>Roman law and laws based upon it have a very deep and rich history that I won’t even attempt to summarize. </p> 
      <p>It is sufficient for present purposes to say the <a href="https://www.law.lsu.edu/clo/digest-online/" target="_blank"><em>Digest of the Civil Laws now in Force in the Territory of Orleans</em></a> (online version, English/French) was enacted in 1808.</p> 
      <p>A scholarly dispute arose (1971-1972) between Professor Batiza (Tulane), who considered the <em>Digest</em> to reflect the French civil code and Professor Pascal (LSU), who argued that despite quoting the French civil code quite liberally, that the redactors intended to codify the Spanish civil law in force at the time of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" target="_blank">Louisiana Purchase</a>.</p> 
      <p>The Batiza vs. Pascal debate was carried out at length and in public:</p> 
      <p>Batiza, <em>The Louisiana Civil Code of 1808: Its Actual Sources and Present Relevance</em>, 46 TUL. L. REV. 4 (1971); Pascal, <em>Sources of the Digest of 1808: A Reply to Professor Batiza</em>, 46 TUL.L.REV. 603 (1972); Sweeney, <em>Tournament of Scholars over the Sources of the Civil Code of 1808</em>, 46 TUL. L. REV. 585 (1972); Batiza, <em>Sources of the Civil Code of 1808, Facts and Speculation: A Rejoinder</em>, 46 TUL. L. REV. 628 (1972).</p> 
      <p>I could not find any freely available copies of those articles online. (Don’t encourage paywalls accessing such material. Find it at your local law library.)</p> 
      <p>There are a couple of secondary articles that discuss the dispute: A.N. Yiannopoulos, <a href="http://www.law.tulane.edu/uploadedFiles/Institutes_and_Centers/Eason_Weinmann/v01i01-Yiannopoulos(1).pdf" target="_blank"><em>The Civil Codes of Louisiana</em></a>, 1 CIV. L. COMMENT. 1, 1 (2008) at http://www.civil-law.org/v01i01-Yiannopoulos.pdf, and John W. Cairns, <a href="https://www2.southeastern.edu/Academics/Faculty/jbell/delavergne.pdf" target="_blank" <em>The de la Vergne Volume and the <em>Digest</em> of 1808</a>, 24 Tulane European &amp; Civil Law Forum 31 (2009), which are freely available online. </p> 
      <p>You won’t get the full details from the secondary articles but they do capture some of the flavor of the original dispute. I can report (happily) that over time, Pascal’s position has prevailed. Textual history is more complex than rote counting techniques can capture. </p> 
      <p>A far more complex case of “text similarity” than Lincoln addresses in the Textreuse package, but once you move beyond freshman/doctoral plagiarism, the “interesting cases” are all complicated.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=222" rel="category">R</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1125" rel="category">Similarity Retrieval</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1390" rel="category">Text Mining</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Editors’ Choice: An Introduction to the Textreuse Package [+ A Counter Example]</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-64679 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-k-nearest-neighbors category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-64679"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=64679" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Fast k-NN search">Fast k-NN search</a></h3> 
     <small>Wednesday, September 23rd, 2015</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://arxiv.org/abs/1509.06957" target="_blank">Fast k-NN search</a> by <a href="http://arxiv.org/find/stat/1/au:+Hyvonen_V/0/1/0/all/0/1" target="_blank">Ville Hyvönen</a>, <a href="http://arxiv.org/find/stat/1/au:+Pitkanen_T/0/1/0/all/0/1" target="_blank">Teemu Pitkänen</a>, <a href="http://arxiv.org/find/stat/1/au:+Tasoulis_S/0/1/0/all/0/1" target="_blank">Sotiris Tasoulis</a>, <a href="http://arxiv.org/find/stat/1/au:+Wang_L/0/1/0/all/0/1" target="_blank">Liang Wang</a>, <a href="http://arxiv.org/find/stat/1/au:+Roos_T/0/1/0/all/0/1" target="_blank">Teemu Roos</a>, <a href="http://arxiv.org/find/stat/1/au:+Corander_J/0/1/0/all/0/1" target="_blank">Jukka Corander</a>.</p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Random projection trees have proven to be effective for approximate nearest neighbor searches in high dimensional spaces where conventional methods are not applicable due to excessive usage of memory and computational time. We show that building multiple trees on the same data can improve the performance even further, without significantly increasing the total computational cost of queries when executed in a modern parallel computing environment. Our experiments identify suitable parameter values to achieve accurate searches with extremely fast query times, while also retaining a feasible complexity for index construction.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Not a quick read but an important one if you want to use multiple dimensions for calculation of similarity or sameness between two or more topics.</p> 
      <p>The technique requires you to choose a degree of similarity that works for your use case.</p> 
      <p>This paper makes a nice jumping off point for discussing how much precision does a particular topic map application need? Absolute precision is possible, but only in a limited number of cases and I suspect at high cost.</p> 
      <p>For some use cases, such as searching for possible suspects in crimes, some lack of precision is necessary to build up a large enough pool of suspects to include the guilty parties.</p> 
      <p>Any examples of precision and topic maps that come to mind? </p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1553" rel="category">K-Nearest-Neighbors</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Fast k-NN search</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-62316 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-parallel-programming category-pattern-recognition category-probabilistic-programming category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-62316"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=62316" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Breaking the Similarity Bottleneck">Breaking the Similarity Bottleneck</a></h3> 
     <small>Saturday, May 9th, 2015</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://dx.doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0124176" target="_blank">Ultra-Fast Data-Mining Hardware Architecture Based on Stochastic Computing</a> by Antoni Morro, et al.</p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Minimal hardware implementations able to cope with the processing of large amounts of data in reasonable times are highly desired in our information-driven society. In this work we review the application of stochastic computing to probabilistic-based pattern-recognition analysis of huge database sets. The proposed technique consists in the hardware implementation of a parallel architecture implementing a similarity search of data with respect to different pre-stored categories. We design pulse-based stochastic-logic blocks to obtain an efficient pattern recognition system. The proposed architecture speeds up the screening process of huge databases by a factor of 7 when compared to a conventional digital implementation using the same hardware area.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>I haven’t included the hyperlinks, but:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p> …<br> In this work we present a highly efficient methodology for data mining based on probabilistic processing. High dimensional data is inherently complex in clustering, classification and similarity search [15]. The proposed approach is evaluated showing its application to a similarity search over a huge database. Most data mining algorithms use similarity search as a subroutine core [16–18], and thus the time taken for this task is the bottleneck of virtually all data mining algorithms [19]. Similarity search plays a fundamental role in many data mining and machine learning problems, e.g. text categorization [20], collaborative filtering [21], time-series analysis [22,23], protein sequencing [24] or any application-specific task as petroglyphs comparison [25]. At the same time, the mining of huge datasets implies the use of large computer clusters [26,27]. The proposed approach based on the use of probabilistic processing shows large improvements in terms of hardware resources when compared with conventional solutions.<br> … </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Sorry they omitted topic maps but what is a merging criteria if it isn’t a type of “similarity?”</p> 
      <p>From the conclusion:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p> …<br> This implementation uses less hardware resources than conventional digital methodologies (based on binary and not probabilistic logic) and is able to process the order of 13GBytes of information per second (in contrast to the estimated 2GBytes/s of speed that could be achieved by the conventional implementation using the same hardware area). With the 12-dimensional space used to allocate each vector in the example shown in this paper we obtain the order of 1 billion of comparisons per second. A patent application has been done for this new mining methodology [32].<br> … </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The <a href="http://www.google.com/patents/WO2014027126A1?cl=es" target="_blank">patent</a> was filed in Spanish but English and French auto-translations are available. </p> 
      <p>Hopefully the patent will be used in such a way as to promote widespread implementation of this technique. </p> 
      <p>I could stand 1 billion comparisons a second, quite easily. Interactive development of merging algorithms anyone? </p> 
      <p>I first saw this in a tweet by <a href="https://twitter.com/sclopit" target="_blank">Stefano Bertolo</a>.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=313" rel="category">Parallel Programming</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=107" rel="category">Pattern Recognition</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=413" rel="category">Probabilistic Programming</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Breaking the Similarity Bottleneck</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-62220 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-latent-dirichlet-allocation-lda category-similarity category-topic-maps category-topic-models-lda"> 
     <h3 id="post-62220"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=62220" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Topic Extraction and Bundling of Related Scientific Articles">Topic Extraction and Bundling of Related Scientific Articles</a></h3> 
     <small>Wednesday, May 6th, 2015</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://arxiv.org/abs/1212.5423" target="_blank">Topic Extraction and Bundling of Related Scientific Articles</a> by <a href="http://arxiv.org/find/cs/1/au:+Parambath_S/0/1/0/all/0/1" target="_blank">Shameem A Puthiya Parambath</a>.</p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Automatic classification of scientific articles based on common characteristics is an interesting problem with many applications in digital library and information retrieval systems. Properly organized articles can be useful for automatic generation of taxonomies in scientific writings, textual summarization, efficient information retrieval etc. Generating article bundles from a large number of input articles, based on the associated features of the articles is tedious and computationally expensive task. In this report we propose an automatic two-step approach for topic extraction and bundling of related articles from a set of scientific articles in real-time. For topic extraction, we make use of Latent Dirichlet Allocation (LDA) topic modeling techniques and for bundling, we make use of hierarchical agglomerative clustering techniques.</p> 
       <p>We run experiments to validate our bundling semantics and compare it with existing models in use. We make use of an online crowdsourcing marketplace provided by Amazon called Amazon Mechanical Turk to carry out experiments. We explain our experimental setup and empirical results in detail and show that our method is advantageous over existing ones. </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>On “bundling” from the introduction:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Effective grouping of data requires a precise definition of closeness between a pair of data items and the notion of closeness always depend on the data and the problem context. Closeness is defined in terms of similarity of the data pairs which in turn is measured in terms of dissimilarity or distance between pair of items. In this report we use the term similarity,dissimilarity and distance to denote the measure of closeness between data items. Most of the bundling scheme start with identifying the common attributes(metadata) of the data set, here scientific articles, and create bundling semantics based on the combination of these attributes. Here we suggest a two step algorithm to bundle scientific articles. In the first step we group articles based on the latent topics in the documents and in the second step we carry out agglomerative hierarchical clustering based on the inter-textual distance and co-authorship similarity between articles. We run experiments to validate the bundling semantics and to compare it with content only based similarity. We used 19937 articles related to Computer Science from arviv [htt12a] for our experiments.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Is a “bundle” the same thing as a topic that represents “all articles on subject X?” </p> 
      <p>I have seen a number of topic map examples that use the equivalent <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proper_noun" target="_blank">proper noun</a>, a proper subject, that is a singular and unique subject.</p> 
      <p>But there is no reason why I could not have a topic that represents all the articles on deep learning written in 2014, for example. Methods such as the bundling techniques described here could prove to be quite useful in such cases.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=272" rel="category">Latent Dirichlet Allocation (LDA)</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=4" rel="category">Topic Maps</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=273" rel="category">Topic Models (LDA)</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Topic Extraction and Bundling of Related Scientific Articles</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-59273 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-clustering category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-59273"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=59273" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to When Similarity Breaks Down">When Similarity Breaks Down</a></h3> 
     <small>Sunday, January 4th, 2015</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><iframe width="450" height="253" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/R_fZNQM-2o4?feature=oembed" frameborder="0" allowfullscreen></iframe></p> 
      <p>From the description:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Clustering data is a fundamental technique in data mining and machine learning. The basic problem can be specified as follows: “Given a set of data, partition the data into a set of groups so that each member of a given group is as similar as possible to the other members of that group and as dissimilar as possible to members of other groups”. In this talk I will try to unpack some of the complexities inherent in this seemingly straightforward description. Specifically, I will discuss some of the issues involved in measuring similarity and try to provide some intuitions into the decisions that need to be made when using such metrics to cluster data.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p><a href="http://nbviewer.ipython.org/github/BartBaddeley/PyDataTalk-2014/blob/master/PyDataTalk.ipynb" target="_blank">iPython notebook</a>, useful because the slides don’t show up well in the video. (Be aware the github link is broken.)</p> 
      <p>Nothing new but Bart does a good job of pointing out that similarity breaks down with high dimensionality. The more dimensions that you are representing in Euclidian space, the less distance there will be along some of those dimensions. </p> 
      <p>At our evening meal today, I was telling my wife about how similarity breaks down under high dimensionality and she recalled a story about how an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autism" target="_blank">autistic child</a> was able to distinguish <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dog" target="_blank">dogs</a> from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cat" target="_blank">cats</a>. </p> 
      <p>The child had struggled to make the distinction for a number of years and then one day succeeded and was able to distinguish dogs from cats.</p> 
      <p>Think of distinguishing dogs and cats as a high dimensionality problem:</p> 
      <table> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <td><strong>Dimension</strong></td> 
         <td><strong>Cat</strong></td> 
         <td><strong>Dog</strong></td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>4 legs</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>2 eyes</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>2 ears</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>fur</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>tail</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>color</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>mammals</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>pet</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>jumps</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>runs</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>plays</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>toys</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
         <td>Y</td> 
        </tr> 
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>There’s twelve that a child would notice. It could go a lot higher with more technical information. </p> 
      <p>The high number of dimensions won’t help an autistic child distinguish dogs from cats. Not likely to help a machine algorithm either. </p> 
      <p>When the child did learn how to distinguish dogs from cats, they were asked what had helped them tell the difference between dogs and cats? </p> 
      <p>The child responded that they looked only at the end of the nose of any animal presented as a dog or a cat to tell the difference. In machine learning lingo, they reduced all the dimensions above and many others to a single one. That one dimension was enough to enable them to reliably tell dogs from cats. </p> 
      <p>Like you, I had to go find images to see if this was possible:</p> 
      <p><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/220px-Kittyply_edit1.jpg"><img src="http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/220px-Kittyply_edit1.jpg" alt="220px-Kittyply_edit1" width="220" height="147" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-59277"></a></p> 
      <p><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/220px-Border_Collie_liver_portrait.jpg"><img src="http://tm.durusau.net/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/220px-Border_Collie_liver_portrait.jpg" alt="220px-Border_Collie_liver_portrait" width="220" height="211" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-59278"></a></p> 
      <p>Do you see it?</p> 
      <p>The shape of a cat’s nose, just the end of it, is a distinct T shape. </p> 
      <p>The shape of a dog’s nose, just the end of it, is round. </p> 
      <p>Every time. T versus round.</p> 
      <p>I can’t think of a better illustration of breaking down of similarity in high dimensions or of the power of dimensional reduction. </p> 
      <p>A topic for another day but it also makes me wonder about dynamically choosing dimensions along which to identify subjects. </p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=114" rel="category">Clustering</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on When Similarity Breaks Down</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-57028 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-natural-language-processing category-semantics category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-57028"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=57028" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to ADW (Align, Disambiguate and Walk) [Semantic Similarity]">ADW (Align, Disambiguate and Walk) [Semantic Similarity]</a></h3> 
     <small>Tuesday, October 14th, 2014</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="https://github.com/pilehvar/ADW" target="_blank">ADW (Align, Disambiguate and Walk) version 1.0</a> by Mohammad Taher Pilehvar.</p> 
      <p>From the webpage:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>This package provides a Java implementation of ADW, a state-of-the-art semantic similarity approach that enables the comparison of lexical items at different lexical levels: from senses to texts. For more details about the approach please refer to: <a href="http://wwwusers.di.uniroma1.it/~navigli/pubs/ACL_2013_Pilehvar_Jurgens_Navigli.pdf" target="_blank">http://wwwusers.di.uniroma1.it/~navigli/pubs/ACL_2013_Pilehvar_Jurgens_Navigli.pdf</a></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The abstract for the paper reads:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Semantic similarity is an essential component of many Natural Language Processing applications. However, prior methods for computing semantic similarity often operate at different levels, e.g., single words or entire documents, which requires adapting the method for each data type. We present a unified approach to semantic similarity that operates at multiple levels, all the way from comparing word senses to comparing text documents. Our method leverages a common probabilistic representation over word senses in order to compare different types of linguistic data. This unified representation shows state-of-the-art performance on three tasks: semantic textual similarity, word similarity, and word sense coarsening.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p><a href="http://lcl.uniroma1.it/adw/" target="_blank">Online Demo</a>.</p> 
      <p>The strength of this approach is the use of multiple levels of semantic similarity. It relies on <a href="http://wordnet.princeton.edu/" target="_blank">WordNet</a> but the authors promise to extend their approach to named entities and other tokens not appearing in WordNet (like your company or industry’s internal vocabulary).</p> 
      <p>The bibliography of the paper cites much of the recent work in this area so that will be an added bonus for perusing the paper. </p> 
      <p>I first saw this in a tweet by <a href="http://twitter.com/kdnuggets" target="_blank">Gregory Piatetsky</a>.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=144" rel="category">Natural Language Processing</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=44" rel="category">Semantics</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on ADW (Align, Disambiguate and Walk) [Semantic Similarity]</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-56605 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-algorithms category-recommendation category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-56605"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=56605" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Twitter open sourced a recommendation algorithm for massive datasets">Twitter open sourced a recommendation algorithm for massive datasets</a></h3> 
     <small>Wednesday, September 24th, 2014</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="https://gigaom.com/2014/09/24/twitter-open-sourced-a-recommendation-algorithm-for-massive-datasets/">Twitter open sourced a recommendation algorithm for massive datasets</a> by Derrick Harris.</p> 
      <p>From the post:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Late last month, <a href="https://blog.twitter.com/2014/all-pairs-similarity-via-dimsum">Twitter open sourced an algorithm</a> that’s designed to ease the computational burden on systems trying to recommend content — contacts, articles, products, whatever — across seemingly endless sets of possibilities. Called DIMSUM, short for Dimension Independent Matrix Square using MapReduce (rolls off the tongue, no?), the algorithm trims the list of potential combinations to a reasonable number, so other recommendation algorithms can run in a reasonable amount of time.</p> 
       <p>Reza Zadeh, the former <a href="http://search.gigaom.com/company/twitter/">Twitter</a> data scientist and current Stanford consulting professor who helped create the algorithm, describes it in terms of the famous handshake problem. Two people in a room? One handshake; no problem. Ten people in a room? Forty-five handshakes; still doable. However, he explained, “The number of handshakes goes up quadratically … That makes the problem very difficult when x is a million.”</p> 
       <p>Twitter claims 271 million active users.</p> 
       <p>DIMSUM works primarily in two different areas: (1) matching promoted ads with the right users, and (2) suggesting similar people to follow after users follow someone. Running through all the possible combinations would take days even on a large cluster of machines, Zadeh said, but sampling the user base using DIMSUM takes significantly less time and significantly fewer machines.<br> … </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The “similarity” of two or more people or bits of content is a variation on the merging rules of the <a href="http://www.isotopicmaps.org/sam/">TMDM</a>. </p> 
      <p>In recommendation language, two or more topics are “similar” if:</p> 
      <blockquote> 
       <ul> 
        <li>at least one equal string in their [subject identifiers] properties,</li> 
        <li>at least one equal string in their [item identifiers] properties,</li> 
        <li>at least one equal string in their [subject locators] properties,</li> 
        <li>an equal string in the [subject identifiers] property of the one topic item and the [item identifiers] property of the other, or</li> 
        <li>the same information item in their [reified] properties.</li> 
       </ul> 
       <p><strong>TMDM 5.3.5 Properties</strong> </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The TMDM says “equal” and not “similar” but the point being that you can arbitrarily decide on how “similar” two or more topics must be in order to trigger merging. </p> 
      <p>That realization opens up the entire realm of “similarity” and “recommendation” algorithms and techniques for application to topic maps.</p> 
      <p>Which brings us back to the algorithm just open sourced by Twitter.</p> 
      <p>With DIMSUM, you don’t have to do a brute force topic by topic comparison for merging purposes. Some topics will not meet a merging “threshold” and not be considered by merging routines. </p> 
      <p>Of course, with the TMDM, merging being either true or false, you may be stuck with brute force. Suggestions?</p> 
      <p>But if you have other similarity measures, you may be able to profit from DIMSUM. </p> 
      <p>BTW, I would not follow #dimsum on Twitter because it is apparently a type of dumpling. ?</p> 
      <p><strong>Update:</strong> <a href="https://issues.apache.org/jira/browse/SPARK-2885" target="_blank">All-pairs similarity via DIMSUM</a> DIMSUM has been implemented in Spark!</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=368" rel="category">Algorithms</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=494" rel="category">Recommendation</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Twitter open sourced a recommendation algorithm for massive datasets</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-55816 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-algorithms category-linguistics category-modeling category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-55816"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=55816" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Improving sparse word similarity models…">Improving sparse word similarity models…</a></h3> 
     <small>Tuesday, August 26th, 2014</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://acl2014.org/acl2014/P14-2/pdf/P14-2049.pdf" target="_blank">Improving sparse word similarity models with asymmetric measures</a> by Jean Mark Gawron.</p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>We show that asymmetric models based on Tversky (1977) improve correlations with human similarity judgments and nearest neighbor discovery for both frequent and middle-rank words. In accord with Tversky’s discovery that asymmetric similarity judgments arise when comparing sparse and rich representations, improvement on our two tasks can be traced to heavily weighting the feature bias toward the rarer word when comparing high- and mid- frequency words.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>From the introduction:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>A key assumption of most models of similarity is that a similarity relation is symmetric. This assumption is foundational for some conceptions, such as the idea of a similarity space, in which similarity is the inverse of distance; and it is deeply embedded into many of the algorithms that build on a similarity relation among objects, such as clustering algorithms. The symmetry assumption is not, however, universal, and it is not essential to all applications of similarity, especially when it comes to modeling human similarity judgments.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>What assumptions underlie your “similarity” measures? </p> 
      <p>Not that we can get away from “assumptions” but are your assumptions based on evidence or are they unexamined assumptions?</p> 
      <p>Do you know of any general techniques for discovering assumptions in algorithms? </p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=368" rel="category">Algorithms</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=475" rel="category">Linguistics</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=863" rel="category">Modeling</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Improving sparse word similarity models…</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-53525 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-hashing category-similarity category-similarity-retrieval"> 
     <h3 id="post-53525"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=53525" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to [O]ne Billion Tweets">[O]ne Billion Tweets</a></h3> 
     <small>Saturday, May 31st, 2014</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://istc-bigdata.org/plsh/docs/plsh_paper.pdf">Streaming Similarity Search over one Billion Tweets using Parallel Locality-Sensitive Hashing</a> by Narayanan Sundaram, <em>et al.</em></p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Finding nearest neighbors has become an important operation on databases, with applications to text search, multimedia indexing, and many other areas. One popular algorithm for similarity search, especially for high dimensional data (where spatial indexes like kd-trees do not perform well) is Locality Sensitive Hashing (LSH), an approximation algorithm for finding similar objects.</p> 
       <p>In this paper, we describe a new variant of LSH, called Parallel LSH (PLSH) designed to be extremely efficient, capable of scaling out on multiple nodes and multiple cores, and which supports high-throughput streaming of new data. Our approach employs several novel ideas, including: cache-conscious hash table layout, using a 2-level merge algorithm for hash table construction; an efficient algorithm for duplicate elimination during hash-table querying; an insert-optimized hash table structure and efficient data expiration algorithm for streaming data; and a performance model that accurately estimates performance of the algorithm and can be used to optimize parameter settings. We show that on a workload where we perform similarity search on a dataset of<br> &gt; 1 Billion tweets, with hundreds of millions of new tweets per day, we can achieve query times of 1–2.5 ms. We show that this is an order of magnitude faster than existing indexing schemes, such as inverted indexes. To the best of our knowledge, this is the fastest implementation of LSH, with table construction times up to 3:7x faster and query times that are 8:3x faster than a basic implementation.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>In the introduction, the authors report “…typical queries taking 1-2.5ms. In comparison to other text search schemes, such as inverted indexes, our approach is an order of magnitude faster.”</p> 
      <p>I looked but did not find any open-source code for PLSH.</p> 
      <p>Caution: If you search for other research, the string “PLSH” is unlikely to be helpful. One my first search I found:</p> 
      <ul> 
       <li>PL/sh is a procedural language handler for PostgreSQL</li> 
       <li>Partia Liberale Shqiptare (Albanian Liberal Party, Kosovo)</li> 
       <li>Pet Loss Support Hotline</li> 
       <li>Promised Land Spanish Horses</li> 
       <li>Polish courses (Abbreviation at Brown University)</li> 
       <li>Point Loma High School</li> 
      </ul> 
     </div>

     <h3 id="post-53024"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=53024" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to The history of the vector space model">The history of the vector space model</a></h3> 
     <small>Wednesday, May 14th, 2014</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://geomblog.blogspot.com/2014/05/the-history-of-vector-space-model.html" target="_blank">The history of the vector space model</a> by Suresh Venkatasubramanian.</p> 
      <p>From the post:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Gerald Salton is generally credited with the invention of the vector space model: the idea that we could represent a document as a vector of keywords and use things like cosine similarity and dimensionality reduction to compare documents and represent them.</p> 
       <p>But the path to this modern interpretation was a lot twistier than one might think. David Dubin wrote an article in 2004 titled ‘<a href="https://www.ideals.illinois.edu/handle/2142/1697" target="_blank">The Most Influential Paper Gerard Salton Never Wrote</a>‘. In it, he points out that most citations that refer to the vector space model refer to a paper that doesn’t actually exist (hence the title). Taking that as a starting point, he then traces the lineage of the ideas in Salton’s work.<br> …</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Suresh summarizes some of the discoveries made by Dubin in his post but this sounds like an interesting research project. To take Dubin’s article as a starting point and follow the development of the vector space model. </p> 
      <p>Particularly since it is used so often for “similarity.” Understanding the mathematics is good, understanding how that particular model was arrived at would be even better.</p> 
      <p>Enjoy!</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=663" rel="category">Vector Space Model (VSM)</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on The history of the vector space model</span></span></p> 
    </div>

     <h3 id="post-52885"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=52885" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to …Locality Sensitive Hashing for Unstructured Data">…Locality Sensitive Hashing for Unstructured Data</a></h3> 
     <small>Friday, May 9th, 2014</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://www.datasciencecentral.com/profiles/blogs/practical-applications-of-locality-sensitive-hashing-for">Practical Applications of Locality Sensitive Hashing for Unstructured Data</a> by Jake Drew.</p> 
      <p>From the post:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>The purpose of this article is to demonstrate how the practical Data Scientist can implement a Locality Sensitive Hashing system from start to finish in order to drastically reduce the search time typically required in high dimensional spaces when finding similar items. Locality Sensitive Hashing accomplishes this efficiency by exponentially reducing the amount of data required for storage when collecting features for comparison between similar item sets. In other words, Locality Sensitive Hashing successfully reduces a high dimensional feature space while still retaining a random permutation of relevant features which research has shown can be used between data sets to determine an accurate approximation of Jaccard similarity [2,3].</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Complete with code and references no less!</p> 
      <p>How “similar” do two items need to be to count as the same item?</p> 
      <p>If two libraries own a physical copy of the same book, for some purposes they are distinct items but for annotations/reviews, you could treat them as one item. </p> 
      <p>If that sounds like a topic map-like question, your right!</p> 
      <p>What measures of similarity are you applying to what subjects? </p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=327" rel="category">Hashing</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1562" rel="category">Jaccard Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=13" rel="category">Subject Identity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on …Locality Sensitive Hashing for Unstructured Data</span></span></p>

     <h3 id="post-50414"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=50414" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Open and transparent altmetrics for discovery">Open and transparent altmetrics for discovery</a></h3> 
     <small>Sunday, February 9th, 2014</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://science.okfn.org/2013/12/09/open-and-transparent-altmetrics-for-discovery/" target="blank">Open and transparent altmetrics for discovery</a> by Peter Kraker.</p> 
      <p>From the post:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Altmetrics are a hot topic in scientific community right now. Classic citation-based indicators such as the impact factor are amended by alternative metrics generated from online platforms. Usage statistics (downloads, readership) are often employed, but links, likes and shares on the web and in social media are considered as well. The altmetrics promise, as laid out in the excellent <a href="http://altmetrics.org/manifesto" target="blank">manifesto</a>, is that they assess impact quicker and on a broader scale. </p> 
       <p>The main focus of altmetrics at the moment is evaluation of scientific output. Examples are the <a href="http://www.plosone.org/article/metrics/info%3Adoi%2F10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0004803" target="blank">article-level metrics in PLOS journals</a>, and the <a href="http://www.altmetric.com/details.php?domain=pubs.rsc.org&amp;citation_id=843656" target="blank">Altmetric donut</a>. <a href="http://impactstory.org/" target="blank">ImpactStory</a> has a slightly different focus, as it aims to evaluate the oeuvre of an author rather than an individual paper. </p> 
       <p>This is all good and well, but in my opinion, altmetrics have a huge potential for discovery that goes beyond rankings of top papers and researchers. A potential that is largely untapped so far. </p> 
       <p>How so? To answer this question, it is helpful to shed a little light on the history of citation indices.<br> …. </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Peter observes that co-citation is a measure of subject similarity, without the need to use the same terminology (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science_Citation_Index" target="blank">Science Citation Index</a>). Peter discovered in his PhD research that co-readership is also an indicator of subject similarity.</p> 
      <p>But more research is needed on co-readership to make it into a reproducible and well understood measure.</p> 
      <p>Peter is appealing for data sets suitable for this research. </p> 
      <p>It is subject similarity at the document level but if as useful as co-citation analysis has proven to be, it will be well worth the effort. </p> 
      <p>Help out if you are able. </p> 
      <p>I first saw this in a tweet by <a href="https://twitter.com/jasonpriem">Jason Priem</a>.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1422" rel="category">Citation Analysis</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Open and transparent altmetrics for discovery</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-50197 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-bioinformatics category-gpu category-similarity category-similarity-retrieval"> 
     <h3 id="post-50197"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=50197" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Parallel implementation of 3D protein structure similarity searches…">Parallel implementation of 3D protein structure similarity searches…</a></h3> 
     <small>Monday, February 3rd, 2014</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://hgpu.org/?p=11353">Parallel implementation of 3D protein structure similarity searches using a GPU and the CUDA</a> by Dariusz Mrozek, Milosz Brozek, and Bozena Malysiak-Mrozek.</p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Searching for similar 3D protein structures is one of the primary processes employed in the field of structural bioinformatics. However, the computational complexity of this process means that it is constantly necessary to search for new methods that can perform such a process faster and more efficiently. Finding molecular substructures that complex protein structures have in common is still a challenging task, especially when entire databases containing tens or even hundreds of thousands of protein structures must be scanned. Graphics processing units (GPUs) and general purpose graphics processing units (GPGPUs) can perform many time-consuming and computationally demanding processes much more quickly than a classical CPU can. In this paper, we describe the GPU-based implementation of the CASSERT algorithm for 3D protein structure similarity searching. This algorithm is based on the two-phase alignment of protein structures when matching fragments of the compared proteins. The GPU (GeForce GTX 560Ti: 384 cores, 2GB RAM) implementation of CASSERT (“GPU-CASSERT”) parallelizes both alignment phases and yields an average 180-fold increase in speed over its CPU-based, single-core implementation on an Intel Xeon E5620 (2.40GHz, 4 cores). In this paper, we show that massive parallelization of the 3D structure similarity search process on many-core GPU devices can reduce the execution time of the process, allowing it to be performed in real time. GPU-CASSERT is available at: <a href="http://zti.polsl.pl/dmrozek/science/gpucassert/cassert.htm">http://zti.polsl.pl/dmrozek/science/gpucassert/cassert.htm</a>.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Seventeen pages of heavy sledding but an average of 180-fold increase in speed? That’s worth the effort.</p> 
      <p>Sorry, I got distracted. How difficult did you say your subject similarity/identity problem was? ?</p> 
     </div>

     <h3 id="post-48259"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=48259" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Using Lucene Similarity in Item-Item Recommenders">Using Lucene Similarity in Item-Item Recommenders</a></h3> 
     <small>Saturday, December 14th, 2013</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://sujitpal.blogspot.com/2013/12/using-lucene-similarity-in-item-item.html">Using Lucene Similarity in Item-Item Recommenders</a> by Sujit Pal.</p> 
      <p>From the post:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Last week, I implemented 4 (of 5) recommenders from the Programming Assignments of the <a href="https://www.coursera.org/course/recsys">Introduction to Recommender Systems</a> course on Coursera, but using <a href="http://mahout.apache.org/">Apache Mahout</a> and Scala instead of <a href="http://lenskit.grouplens.org/">Lenskit</a> and Java. This week, I implement an Item Item Collaborative Filtering Recommender that uses Lucene (more specifically, Lucene’s <a href="http://cephas.net/blog/2008/03/30/how-morelikethis-works-in-lucene/">More Like This</a> query) as the item similarity provider.</p> 
       <p>By default, Lucene stores document vectors keyed by terms, but can be configured to store term vectors by setting the field attribute TermVector.YES. In case of text documents, words (or terms) are the features which are used to compute similarity between documents. I am using the same <a href="https://github.com/sujitpal/mia-scala-examples/tree/master/data/recsys">dataset</a> as last week, where movies (items) correspond to documents and movie tags correspond to the words. So we build a movie “document” by preprocessing the tags to form individual tokens and concatenating them into a tags field in the index.</p> 
       <p>Three scenarios are covered. The first two are similar to the scenarios covered with the item-item collaborative filtering recommender from last week, where the user is on a movie page, and we need to (a) predict the rating a user would given a specified movie and (b) find movies similar to a given movie. The third scenario is recommending movies to a given user. We describe each algorithm briefly, and how Lucene fits in.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>I’m curious how easy/difficult it would be to re-purpose similarity algorithms to detect common choices in avatar characteristics, acquisitions, interaction with others, goals, etc.?</p> 
      <p>Thinking that while obvious repetitions are easy enough to avoid, gender, age, names, etc., there are other, more subtle characteristics of interaction with others that would be far harder to be aware of. Much less to mask effectively.</p> 
      <p>It would require a lot of data on interaction but I assume that isn’t all that difficult to whistle up on any of the major systems. </p> 
      <p>If you have any pointers to that sort of research, forward them along. I will be posting a collection of pointers and will credit anyone who wants to be credited. </p> 
     </div>
