    </div> 
    <div class="post-47611 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-elasticsearch category-similarity category-similarity-retrieval"> 
     <h3 id="post-47611"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=47611" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Similarity in Elasticsearch">Similarity in Elasticsearch</a></h3> 
     <small>Wednesday, November 27th, 2013</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://www.found.no/foundation/similarity/">Similarity in Elasticsearch</a> by Konrad G. Beiske.</p> 
      <p>From the post:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>A similarity model is a set of abstractions and metrics to define to what extent things are similar. That’s quite a general definition. In this article I will only consider textual similarity. In this context, the uses of similarity models can be divided into two categories: classification of documents, with a finite set of categories where the categories are known; and information retrieval where the problem can be defined as ‘find the the most relevant documents to a given query’. In this article I will look into the latter category.</p> 
       <p>Elasticsearch provides the following similarity models: default, bm25, drf and ib. I have limited the scope of this article to default and bm25. The divergence from randomness and information based similarities may feature in a future article.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Konrad goes on to talk about the default similarity model in Elasticsearch, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tf/idf">Tf/idf</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Okapi_BM25">BM25</a> (aka Okapi BM25), a probabilistic model.</p> 
      <p>He also points the reader to: <a href="http://www.soi.city.ac.uk/~ser/papers/foundations_bm25_review.pdf">The Probabilistic Relevance Framework: BM25 and Beyond</a> for further details on BM25.</p> 
      <p>A good post if you want to learn more about tuning similarity in Elasticsearch.</p> 
      <p>BTW, <a href="http://www.elasticsearch.org/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/current/index-modules-similarity.html">documentation on similarity module for 0.90</a>.</p> 
      <p>While the build-in similarity models offer a lot of mileage no doubt, I am more intrigued by the potential for creating a custom similarity model. </p> 
      <p>As you know, some people think <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_language">English</a> words are just <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_language">English</a> words. Search engines tend to ignore time, social class, context of use, etc., in returning all the “instances” of an English word. </p> 
      <p>That is to say the similarity model for one domain or period could be quite different from the similarity model for another.</p> 
      <p>Domain or period specific similarity models would be difficult to construct and certainly less general.</p> 
      <p>Given the choice, of being easy, general and less accurate versus being harder, less general and more accurate, which would you choose?</p> 
      <p>Does your answer change if you are a consumer looking for the best results or a developer trying to sell “good enough” results? </p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=428" rel="category">ElasticSearch</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1125" rel="category">Similarity Retrieval</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Similarity in Elasticsearch</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-45752 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-cheminformatics category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-45752"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=45752" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Similarity maps…">Similarity maps…</a></h3> 
     <small>Thursday, September 26th, 2013</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://dx.doi.org/10.1186/1758-2946-5-43">Similarity maps – a visualization strategy for molecular fingerprints and machine-learning methods</a> by Sereina Riniker and Gregory A Landrum.</p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Fingerprint similarity is a common method for comparing chemical structures. Similarity is an appealing approach because, with many fingerprint types, it provides intuitive results: a chemist looking at two molecules can understand why they have been determined to be similar. This transparency is partially lost with the fuzzier similarity methods that are often used for scaffold hopping and tends to vanish completely when molecular fingerprints are used as inputs to machine-learning (ML) models. Here we present similarity maps, a straightforward and general strategy to visualize the atomic contributions to the similarity between two molecules or the predicted probability of a ML model. We show the application of similarity maps to a set of dopamine D3 receptor ligands using atom-pair and circular fingerprints as well as two popular ML methods: random forests and na?ve Bayes. An open-source implementation of the method is provided.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>If you are doing topic maps in areas where molecular fingerprints are relevant, this could be quite useful.</p> 
      <p>Despite my usual warnings that semantics are continuous versus the discrete structures treated here, this may also be useful in “fuzzier” areas where topic maps are found.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=134" rel="category">Cheminformatics</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Similarity maps…</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-43836 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-erlang category-searching category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-43836"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=43836" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Sherlock’s Last Case">Sherlock’s Last Case</a></h3> 
     <small>Sunday, July 14th, 2013</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hh4tmDB9iQI">Sherlock’s Last Case</a> by Joe Armstrong.</p> 
      <p>Joe states the Sherlock problem as given one X and millions of Yi’s, “Which Yi is “nearer to X?”</p> 
      <p>For some measure of “nearer,” or as we prefer, similarity. </p> 
      <p>One solution is given in <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/193778553X/">Programming Erlang: Software for a Concurrent World</a>, 2nd ed., 2013, by Joe Armstrong.</p> 
      <p>Joe describes two possibly better solutions in this lecture. </p> 
      <p>Great lecture even if he omits a fundamental weakness in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tf%E2%80%93idf">TF-IDF</a>.</p> 
      <p>From the Wikipedia entry:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Suppose we have a set of English text documents and wish to determine which document is most relevant to the query “the brown cow”. A simple way to start out is by eliminating documents that do not contain all three words “the”, “brown”, and “cow”, but this still leaves many documents. To further distinguish them, we might count the number of times each term occurs in each document and sum them all together; the number of times a term occurs in a document is called its term frequency.</p> 
       <p>However, because the term “the” is so common, this will tend to incorrectly emphasize documents which happen to use the word “the” more frequently, without giving enough weight to the more meaningful terms “brown” and “cow”. The term “the” is not a good keyword to distinguish relevant and non-relevant documents and terms, unlike the less common words “brown” and “cow”. Hence an inverse document frequency factor is incorporated which diminishes the weight of terms that occur very frequently in the document set and increases the weight of terms that occur rarely. </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>For example, TF-IDF would not find a document with “the brown heifer,” for a query of “the brown cow.”</p> 
      <p>TF-IDF does not account for relationships between terms, such as synonymy or polysemy. </p> 
      <p>Juam Ramos states as much in describing the limitations of TF-IDF in: <a href="http://www.cs.rutgers.edu/~mlittman/courses/ml03/iCML03/papers/ramos.pdf">Using TF-IDF to Determine Word Relevance in Document Queries</a>:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Despite its strength, TF-IDF has its limitations. In terms of synonyms, notice that TF-IDF does not make the jump to the relationship between words. Going back to (Berger &amp; Lafferty, 1999), if the user wanted to find information about, say, the word ‘priest’, TF-IDF would not consider documents that might be relevant to the query but instead use the word ‘reverend’. In our own experiment, TF-IDF could not equate the word ‘drug’ with its plural ‘drugs’, categorizing each instead as separate words and slightly decreasing the word’s w<sub>d</sub> value. For large document collections, this could present an escalating problem. </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Ramos cites <a href="http://www.mti.ugm.ac.id/~adji/courses/resources/doctor/scholargoogle/StatisticalTranslation99.pdf">Information Retrieval as Statistical Translation</a> by Adam Berger and John Lafferty to support his comments on synonymy or polysemy.</p> 
      <p>The Berger and Lafferty treat synonymy and polysemy, issues that TF-IDF misses, as statistical translation issues:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Ultimately document retrieval systems must be sophisticated enough to handle polysemy and synonymyto know for instance that pontiff and pope are related terms The eld of statistical translation concerns itself with how to mine large text databases to automatically discover such semantic relations Brown et al [3, 4] showed for instance how a system can learn to associate French terms with their English translations given only a collection of bilingual FrenchEnglish sentences We shall demonstrate how in a similar fashion an IR system can from a collection of documents automatically learn which terms are related and exploit these relations to better nd and rank the documents it returns to the user </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Merging powered by the results of statistical translation? </p> 
      <p>The Berger and Lafferty paper is more than a decade old so I will be running the research forward. </p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=315" rel="category">Erlang</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=3" rel="category">Searching</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Sherlock’s Last Case</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-42172 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-k-nearest-neighbors category-postgresql category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-42172"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=42172" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to K-nearest neighbour search for PostgreSQL [Data Types For Distance Operator]">K-nearest neighbour search for PostgreSQL [Data Types For Distance Operator]</a></h3> 
     <small>Wednesday, May 29th, 2013</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://www.sai.msu.su/~megera/postgres/talks/pgcon-2010-1.pdf">K-nearest neighbour search for PostgreSQL</a> by Oleg Bartunov and Teodor Sigaev.</p> 
      <p>Excellent presentation from PGCon-2010 on the KNN index type in Postgres.</p> 
      <p>And an exception to the rule about wordy slides.</p> 
      <p>Or at least wordy slides are better for non-attendees. </p> 
      <p>KNN uses the <code>&lt;-&gt;</code> distance operator.</p> 
      <p>And the slides say:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>distance operator, should be provided for data type</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Looking at the Postgre documentation (9.3, but same for 9.1 and 9.2), I read:</p> 
      <blockquote> 
       <p>In addition to the typical B-tree search operators, <code>btree_gist</code> also provides index support for <code>&lt;&gt;</code> (“not equals”). This may be useful in combination with an <a href="http://www.postgresql.org/docs/9.1/static/sql-createtable.html#SQL-CREATETABLE-EXCLUDE">exclusion constraint</a>, as described below.</p> 
       <p>Also, for data types for which there is a natural distance metric, <code>btree_gist</code> defines a distance operator <code>&lt;-&gt;</code>, and provides GiST index support for nearest-neighbor searches using this operator. Distance operators are provided for <code>int2</code>, <code>int4</code>, <code>int8</code>, <code>float4</code>, <code>float8</code>, <code>timestamp with time zone</code>, <code>timestamp without time zone</code>, <code>time without time zone</code>, <code>date</code>, <code>interval</code>, <code>oid</code>, and <code>money</code>.</p> 
      </blockquote> 
      <p>What collective subjects would you like to gather up using the defined data types for the distance operator?</p> 
      <p>How would you represent properties to take advantage of the defined data types?</p> 
      <p>Are there other data types that you would define for the distance operator? </p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=1553" rel="category">K-Nearest-Neighbors</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=359" rel="category">PostgreSQL</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on K-nearest neighbour search for PostgreSQL [Data Types For Distance Operator]</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-42146 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-graphs category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-42146"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=42146" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Similarity for Topic Maps?">Similarity for Topic Maps?</a></h3> 
     <small>Tuesday, May 28th, 2013</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://blog.smola.org/post/33412570425/the-weisfeiler-lehman-algorithm-and-estimation-on">The Weisfeiler-Lehman algorithm and estimation on graphs</a> by Alex Smola. </p> 
      <p>From the post:</p> 
      <blockquote> 
       <p>Imagine you have two graphs \(G\) and \(G’\) and you’d like to check how similar they are. If all vertices have unique attributes this is quite easy:</p> 
       <p>FOR ALL vertices \(v \in G \cup G’\) DO</p> 
       <ul> 
        <li>Check that \(v \in G\) and that \(v \in G’\)</li> 
        <li>Check that the neighbors of v are the same in \(G\) and \(G’\)</li> 
       </ul> 
       <p>This algorithm can be carried out in linear time in the size of the graph, alas many graphs do not have vertex attributes, let alone unique vertex attributes. In fact, graph isomorphism, i.e. the task of checking whether two graphs are identical, is a hard problem (it is still an open research question how hard it really is). In this case the above algorithm cannot be used since we have no idea which vertices we should match up.</p> 
       <p>The Weisfeiler-Lehman algorithm is a mechanism for assigning fairly unique attributes efficiently. Note that it isn’t guaranteed to work, as discussed in <a href="http://arxiv.org/abs/1101.5211" title="B. L. Douglas">this paper</a> by Douglas – this would solve the graph isomorphism problem after all. The idea is to assign fingerprints to vertices and their neighborhoods repeatedly. We assume that vertices have an attribute to begin with. If they don’t then simply assign all of them the attribute 1. Each iteration proceeds as follows:</p> 
       <p>(…) </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Something a bit more sophisticated than comparing canonical representations in syntax.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=89" rel="category">Graphs</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Similarity for Topic Maps?</span></span></p> 
    </div> 
    <div class="post-40707 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-graphs category-networks category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-40707"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=40707" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to DELTACON: A Principled Massive-Graph Similarity Function">DELTACON: A Principled Massive-Graph Similarity Function</a></h3> 
     <small>Saturday, April 20th, 2013</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p>DELTACON: A Principled Massive-Graph Similarity Function by Danai Koutra, Joshua T. Vogelstein, Christos Faloutsos.</p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>How much did a network change since yesterday? How different is the wiring between Bob’s brain (a left-handed male) and Alice’s brain (a right-handed female)? Graph similarity with known node correspondence, i.e. the detection of changes in the connectivity of graphs, arises in numerous settings. In this work, we formally state the axioms and desired properties of the graph similarity functions, and evaluate when state-of-the-art methods fail to detect crucial connectivity changes in graphs. We propose DeltaCon, a principled, intuitive, and scalable algorithm that assesses the similarity between two graphs on the same nodes (e.g. employees of a company, customers of a mobile carrier). Experiments on various synthetic and real graphs showcase the advantages of our method over existing similarity measures. Finally, we employ DeltaCon to real applications: (a) we classify people to groups of high and low creativity based on their brain connectivity graphs, and (b) do temporal anomaly detection in the who-emails-whom Enron graph.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>How different is your current topic map from a prior version?</p> 
      <p>Could be an interesting marketing ploy to colorize the distinct portions of the graph.</p> 
      <p>Not to mention using “similarity” to mean the same subject for some purposes. Group subjects come to mind.</p> 
      <p>And for other types of analysis.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=89" rel="category">Graphs</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=104" rel="category">Networks</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on DELTACON: A Principled Massive-Graph Similarity Function</span></span></p>

    <div class="post-38629 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-hashing category-indexing category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-38629"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=38629" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Learning Hash Functions Using Column Generation">Learning Hash Functions Using Column Generation</a></h3> 
     <small>Monday, March 11th, 2013</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://arxiv.org/abs/1303.0339">Learning Hash Functions Using Column Generation</a> by Xi Li, Guosheng Lin, Chunhua Shen, Anton van den Hengel, Anthony Dick.</p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Fast nearest neighbor searching is becoming an increasingly important tool in solving many large-scale problems. Recently a number of approaches to learning data-dependent hash functions have been developed. In this work, we propose a column generation based method for learning data-dependent hash functions on the basis of proximity comparison information. Given a set of triplets that encode the pairwise proximity comparison information, our method learns hash functions that preserve the relative comparison relationships in the data as well as possible within the large-margin learning framework. The learning procedure is implemented using column generation and hence is named CGHash. At each iteration of the column generation procedure, the best hash function is selected. Unlike most other hashing methods, our method generalizes to new data points naturally; and has a training objective which is convex, thus ensuring that the global optimum can be identified. Experiments demonstrate that the proposed method learns compact binary codes and that its retrieval performance compares favorably with state-of-the-art methods when tested on a few benchmark datasets.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Interesting review of hashing techniques. </p> 
      <p>Raises the question of customized similarity (read sameness) hashing algorithms for topic maps.</p> 
      <p>I first saw this in a tweet by <a href="https://twitter.com/sclopit">Stefano Bertolo</a>.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=327" rel="category">Hashing</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=7" rel="category">Indexing</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on Learning Hash Functions Using Column Generation</span></span></p>

     <h3 id="post-35927"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=35927" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Similarity Search and Applications">Similarity Search and Applications</a></h3> 
     <small>Friday, January 18th, 2013</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://www.sisap.org/2013/">International Conference on Similarity Search and Applications (SISAP 2013)</a></p> 
      <p>From the webpage:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>The <em>International Conference on Similarity Search and Applications (SISAP)</em> is an annual forum for researchers and application developers in the area of similarity data management. It aims at the technological problems shared by numerous application domains, such as data mining, information retrieval, computer vision, pattern recognition, computational biology, geography, biometrics, machine learning, and many others that need similarity searching as a necessary supporting service.</p> 
       <p>The SISAP initiative (<a href="http://www.sisap.org">www.sisap.org</a>) aims to become a forum to exchange real-world, challenging and innovative examples of applications, new indexing techniques, common test-beds and benchmarks, source code and up-to-date literature through its web page, serving the similarity search community. Traditionally, SISAP puts emphasis on the distance-based searching, but in general the conference concerns both the effectiveness and efficiency aspects of any similarity search problem.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Dates:</p> 
      <p><strong>Paper submission: April 2013<br> Notification: June 2013<br> Final version: July 2013<br> Conference: October 2, 3, and 4, 2013</strong></p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>The specific topics include, but are not limited to:</p> 
       <ul> 
        <li>Similarity queries – k-NN, range, reverse NN, top-k, etc.</li> 
        <li>Similarity operations – joins, ranking, classification, categorization, filtering, etc.</li> 
        <li>Evaluation techniques for similarity queries and operations</li> 
        <li>Merging/combining multiple similarity modalities</li> 
        <li>Cost models and analysis for similarity data processing</li> 
        <li>Scalability issues and high-performance similarity data management</li> 
        <li>Feature extraction for similarity-based data findability</li> 
        <li>Test collections and benchmarks</li> 
        <li>Performance studies, benchmarks, and comparisons</li> 
        <li>Similarity Search over outsourced data repositories</li> 
        <li>Similarity search cloud services</li> 
        <li>Languages for similarity databases</li> 
        <li>New modes of similarity for complex data understanding</li> 
        <li>Applications of similarity-based operations</li> 
        <li>Image, video, voice, and music (multimedia) retrieval systems</li> 
        <li>Similarity for forensics and security</li> 
       </ul> 
      </blockquote> 
      <p>You should be able to find one or more topics that interest you. ?</p> 
      <p>How similar must two or more references to an entity be before they are identifying the same entity? </p> 
      <p>Or for that matter, is similarity an association between two or more references? </p> 
     </div>

    <div class="post-35665 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-cheminformatics category-similarity"> 
     <h3 id="post-35665"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=35665" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to PubChem3D: conformer ensemble accuracy">PubChem3D: conformer ensemble accuracy</a></h3> 
     <small>Sunday, January 13th, 2013</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://dx.doi.org/10.1186/1758-2946-5-1">PubChem3D: conformer ensemble accuracy</a> by Sunghwan Kim, Evan E Bolton and Stephen H Bryant. (<em>Journal of Cheminformatics</em> 2013, <strong>5</strong>:1 doi:10.1186/1758-2946-5-1)</p> 
      <p>Abstract:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p><strong>Background</strong></p> 
       <p>PubChem is a free and publicly available resource containing substance descriptions and their associated biological activity information. PubChem3D is an extension to PubChem containing computationally-derived three-dimensional (3-D) structures of small molecules. All the tools and services that are a part of PubChem3D rely upon the quality of the 3-D conformer models. Construction of the conformer models currently available in PubChem3D involves a clustering stage to sample the conformational space spanned by the molecule. While this stage allows one to downsize the conformer models to more manageable size, it may result in a loss of the ability to reproduce experimentally determined “bioactive” conformations, for example, found for PDB ligands. This study examines the extent of this accuracy loss and considers its effect on the 3-D similarity analysis of molecules.</p> 
       <p><strong>Results</strong></p> 
       <p>The conformer models consisting of up to 100,000 conformers per compound were generated for 47,123 small molecules whose structures were experimentally determined, and the conformers in each conformer model were clustered to reduce the size of the conformer model to a maximum of 500 conformers per molecule. The accuracy of the conformer models before and after clustering was evaluated using five different measures: root-mean-square distance (RMSD), shape-optimized shape-Tanimoto (STST-opt) and combo-Tanimoto (ComboTST-opt), and color-optimized color-Tanimoto (CTCT-opt) and combo-Tanimoto (ComboTCT-opt). On average, the effect of clustering decreased the conformer model accuracy, increasing the conformer ensemble’s RMSD to the bioactive conformer (by 0.18 +/- 0.12 A), and decreasing the STST-opt, ComboTST-opt, CTCT-opt, and ComboTCT-opt scores (by 0.04 +/- 0.03, 0.16 +/- 0.09, 0.09 +/- 0.05, and 0.15 +/- 0.09, respectively).</p> 
       <p><strong>Conclusion</strong></p> 
       <p>This study shows the RMSD accuracy performance of the PubChem3D conformer models is operating as designed. In addition, the effect of PubChem3D sampling on 3-D similarity measures shows that there is a linear degradation of average accuracy with respect to molecular size and flexibility. Generally speaking, one can likely expect the worst-case minimum accuracy of 90% or more of the PubChem3D ensembles to be 0.75, 1.09, 0.43, and 1.13, in terms of STST-opt, ComboTST-opt, CTCT-opt, and ComboTCT-opt, respectively. This expected accuracy improves linearly as the molecule becomes smaller or less flexible.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>If I were to say, potential shapes of a subject, would that the importance of this work clearer?</p> 
      <p>Wikipedia has this two-liner that may also help: </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>A macromolecule is usually flexible and dynamic. It can change its shape in response to changes in its environment or other factors; each possible shape is called a conformation, and a transition between them is called a <strong>conformational change</strong>. A macromolecular conformational change may be induced by many factors such as a change in temperature, pH, voltage, ion concentration, phosphorylation, or the binding of a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ligand">ligand</a>.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Subjects and the manner of their identification is a very deep and rewarding field of study.</p> 
      <p>An identification method in isolation is no better or worse than any other identification method. </p> 
      <p>Only your requirements (which are also subjects) can help with the process of choosing one or more identification methods over others.</p> 
     </div> 
     <p class="postmetadata"> Posted in <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=134" rel="category">Cheminformatics</a>, <a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?cat=115" rel="category">Similarity</a> | <span>Comments Off<span class="screen-reader-text"> on PubChem3D: conformer ensemble accuracy</span></span></p> 
    </div>

     <h3 id="post-34415"><a href="http://tm.durusau.net/?p=34415" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Music Network Visualization">Music Network Visualization</a></h3> 
     <small>Tuesday, December 11th, 2012</small> 
     <div class="entry"> 
      <p><a href="http://rulesofreason.wordpress.com/2012/12/11/music-network-visualization/">Music Network Visualization</a> by Dimiter Toshkov.</p> 
      <p>From the post:</p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>My music interests have always been rather, hmm…, eclectic. Somehow IDM, ambient, darkwave, triphop, acid jazz, bossa nova, qawali, Mali blues and other more or less obscure genres have managed to happily co-exist in my music collection. The sheer diversity always invited the question whether there is some structure to the collection, or each genre is an island of its own. Sounds like a job for network visualization!</p> 
       <p>Now, there are plenty of music network viz applications on the <a href="http://www.slideshare.net/plamere/using-visualizations-for-music-discovery">web</a>. But they don’t show <strong>my</strong> collection, and just seem unsatisfactory for various reasons. So I decided to craft my own visualization using <em>R</em> and <em>igraph</em>. </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Interesting for the visualization but also the use of similarity measures. </p> 
      <p>The test for identity of a subject, particularly collective subjects, artists “similar” to X, is as unlimited as your imagination.</p>
